Talks with Chinese Monarchists
In 1911, Chinese revolutionaries toppled the Xuantong Emperor, the then two-year old Puyi. This was the culmination of decades of Qing political, economic, and military decline. The Xinhai Revolution involved the conflux of republicanism, radicalism, and ethno-nationalism and an attempt by Yuan Shikai (egged on by Western advisors) to crown himself as Emperor a few years later plunged the nation into a decades long Warlord Era.\n\nPolitical support for the monarchy is limited though opinions have mellowed out due to the chaos of republican rule in the ensuing years. Still, many are wary of attempting to install a monarchy by force and the \

The Reformation of the Republic
Japan has long cultivated influence in China, sifting through various allies and collaborators in hopes of expanding their reach into their more populous neighbor. Various Chinese factions have worked with Japan, either our of genuine Pan-Asian idealism or belief that the two powers could work together against European imperialism or a pragmatic desire to avoid war or total domination by Japan or simply because they wanted or needed Japanese help.\n\nMost pro-Japanese forces ended up aligned with the Fengtian clique or the broader National Pacification Army (Anguojun), with the Zhang Zuolin faction, the Shikan Clique, the Recovery League (the successors of the Anhui Clique), and New Communications Clique all having varying degrees of pro-Japanese sympathies. More directly sponsored by Tokyo were the Concordia Association and the New People's Society.\n\nMore minor Japanese allies include Chen Lianbo and his Merchant government in Guangzhou (Canton), who have sought to deepen their ties with Japan to counteract Zhili and Old Guangxi influence. Similarly, our agents have also worked to court Governor Yan Xishan of Shanxi to our side.\n\nWith the old republic having been destroyed, a new one must be formed - merging competing projects by the highly factionalised Japanese Army, Navy, and civilian government. Federalism in China?
Federalism in China has had a complicated history, with a brief high point in the early 1920s followed by a speedy decline in the ensuing years. Still, it retains its popularity among those who fear the tyranny and corruption of the central government, and the Federalist movement in recent years has absorbed democratic, constitutionalist, and regionalist elements as well.\n\nInstalling a Federalist government in China will likely be viewed with suspicion as a means of dividing and conquering, though local politicians who stand to benefit from such a system may be quick to embrace it. The Federalist movement in China, by its very nature, is politically diverse and diffuse. Liangguang, Yunnan, Hunan, Sichuan, and Zhejiang are perhaps the most notable centers of the Chinese Federalist movement.

A New Revolution in China?
The 1911 Xinhai Revolution was a cataclysmic event in Chinese history and one that opened the door for radical political, social, and economic ideas to flourish in Chinese discourse. But translating ideas into action was difficult and spurred further division among this new generation of socialists, nationalists, and revolutionary republicans.\n\nDr. Sun Yat-sen and his Kuomintang became the foremost revolutionary party during the Warlord era but they were hardly alone. The Guominjun, the Chinese Syndicalist Party, the World Society, and various splinters and independent groups emerged throughout the years, all vying for a chance to reshape China in their image. Forcibly installing revolution onto China is bound to be difficult, but may forever alter the course of the ancient civilisation's history.

Building a Chinese Domestic Defence Industry
One of the few silver linings of decades of civil war is that heavy military spending has built a sturdy foundation for a modern military-industrial complex. However, many barriers remain. Besides the usual suspects of corruption, inexperience and wartime disruption, Chinese soldiers historically have been equipped with foreign designs, if not outright foreign imports, stifling the growth of a domestic arms industry. Furthermore, most of China's vast arsenals are heavily decentralised and poorly integrated, a product of warlord regionalism. As we march towards unification, we must work to capture and synchronise these military production hubs, unleashing the full potential of the Chinese industrial behemoth!
Integrated the Shenyang Arsenal
Integrated the Taiyuan Arsenal
Integrated the Shanghai Arsenal
Integrated the Gongxian Arsenal
Integrated the Guangdong Arsenal
Integrated the Jinling Arsenal
Integrated the Hanyang Arsenal
[CHN_integrate_major_arsenal_scripted_loc]
[CHN_integrate_major_arsenal_desc_scripted_loc]
Integrate Shenyang Arsenal
Shenyang Arsenal is perhaps one of the hallmark achievements of the Fengtian clique, created and maintained with the help of Japanese advisors to maintain Zhang Zuolin's grip over Manchuria and fuel his ambitions of uniting China under his banner. Formally known as the Eastern Three Province Arsenal, it is one of the largest in China and boasts of its ability to produce more arms than all others in China combined across the 1920s. Taking advantage of Dongbei's superior industrial might and rich natural resources, Shenyang Arsenal became a centre for not only production but also creating an extensive military logistical chain (including centralised production of rations and uniforms), as well as weapons development of their own. While Zhang was unable to fully break China's dependence on foreign arms imports, perhaps under our management it will play a valuable role in building a domestic arms industry.
Shenyang Arsenal has been integrated
Integrate Taiyuan Arsenal
Taiyuan Arsenal is a massive complex built in Shanxi's capital by its venerable Governor Yan Xishan. Quoted as saying \
Taiyuan Arsenal has been integrated
Integrate Gongxian Arsenal
In 1914, Yuan Shicai personally oversaw the construction of Gongxian Arsenal in Henan. Taking advantage of its strategic position and easy access to raw materials, Admiral Sa Zhenbing and hired German engineers used purchased American machinery to establish one of the premier modern military arsenals in China. Its relatively intact capture has secured for us a valuable prize in central China, one we must quickly integrate with the rest of our defence industry.
Gongxian Arsenal has been integrated
Integrate Hanyang Arsenal
Hanyang Arsenal is one of the largest, oldest and most important modern Chinese Arsenals. Built in 1891 as Hubei Arsenal, it required significant time and funds diverted from the fleet. Capable of producing artillery, rifles, and ammunition, Hanyang fed a series of armies including the original Xinhai Revolutionaries based in Wuhan. It has achieved fame developing and producing the Hanyang 88 rifle (based on the German Gewehr 1888) and the General Liu Rifle (designed by Hanyang Superintendent General Liu Qingen), some of the most iconic weapons of the Warlord Era.
Hanyang Arsenal has been integrated
Integrate Guangdong Arsenal
First erected by the Viceroy of Liangguang in 1873, the Guangdong Arsenal got its start as the Guangdong Machinery Bureau producing light arms. Gradually, it was expanded and modernised, constructing new facilities and absorbing other arsenals like Zengbu Arms Bureau to create a powerful military industrial complex in Guangdong. The Xinhai Revolution and ensuing wars saw Guangzhou become the centre of a succession of southern governments, and Guangdong Arsenal's role only continued to expand. Assisted by other regional arsenals like at Shijing, Pajiang, and even a dedicated gas-mask factory, the facilities of southern China will be of great service to the nation's cause.
Guangdong Arsenal has been integrated
Integrate Jinling Arsenal
Jinling Machinery Manufacturing Bureau, built atop the old Xitian Temple site by late Qing Dynasty reformer Li Hongzhang in 1865, quickly became one of China's venerable weapons production depots in Eastern China. Referred sometimes as \
Jinling Arsenal has been integrated
Integrate Shanghai Arsenal
Established in 1865 during the Self-Strengthening Movement by Li Hongzhang and Zeng Guofan, the Jiangnan Manufacturing Bureau in Shanghai was created to supply modern firearms for the Qing Dynasty. Despite facing political turmoil over decades, the factory underwent significant expansions and developments. Renamed the Shanghai Arsenal in 1917, it emerged as one of the most productive manufacturers of machine tools and modern weaponry, including heavy machine guns, mortars, field guns, and even antiaircraft guns.
Shanghai Arsenal has been integrated

The large-scale military production facilities here are of the utmost quality, or at least could be with sufficient investment and attention by our government. We must work to amalgamate them into our broader production line.

Integrate Jinan Arsenal
Created as the Shandong Machinery Bureau by Governor Ding Baozhen in 1875, it acquired machinery needed to produce weapons a few years later in 1883. It began producing a series of light arms derived from western designs, quickly expanding its industrial base to producing increasingly large amounts of rifles and other infantry equipment. Renamed Shandong Arsenal by the Beiyang Government, it saw further production and its final name as Jinan Arsenal under Zhang Zhongchang's rule.
Integrate Chengdu Arsenal
The disruption caused by various wars in Eastern China, including recent developments following the collapse of Qing authority, have pushed some relocations of production infrastructure towards the hinterlands. While the extent to which these moves were planned versus hasty opportunism is debated, Chengdu has been a willing recipient of these evacuations, creating a burgeoning defence sector of its own.
Integrate Guizhou Arsenal
The Guizhou Arsenal was founded in August 1926 in Guiyang by Zhou Xichen, through the merger of the Chishui Arsenal and the Yanhe Gun Factory, which were initially established by Zhou in 1923 and 1924 respectively, to manufacture pistols and rifles for his power struggle in Guizhou. Joined by a smokeless powder factory which greatly bolstered production, the Guizhou Arsenal now produced machine guns, grenades, shells and even mines for Zhou Xichen's armies.
Integrate Guangxi Arsenal
Originally established in Longcheng in 1899 to produce weapons for the Chinese army in the southwestern frontier, the Guangxi Machinery Bureau was burnt in a fire accident in 1914 and was relocated to Nanning. It was first used by Lu Rongting to arm his Guangxi army and later taken over by Li Zongren and Huang Shaohong to create the Guangxi Arsenal by merging their respective arms factories in 1924 to produce rifles for their soldiers.
Integrate Small Arms Arsenal at [FROM.GetName]
[FROM.GetName] has become quite a centre for small arms production, churning out a sizeable amount of rifles, grenades, and ammunition for local armies over the years. Having become its newest masters, it is critical that we make the most out of their expertise for the good of the nation.

Yunnan Arsenal
Formed in 1884 as the Yunnan Machinery Bureau in Kunming to repair and produce firearms, the Yunnan Arsenal began to produce modern rifles in 1905. Renamed after the collapse of the Qing, it began producing grenades and small shells and provided arms to the Yunnan Army during the Second Revolution and the National Protection War under the local administration of Yunnan, which expanded the arsenal in 1927.
Expand Hunan Arsenal
As the military needs of the Zhili clique grew, some experts from Gongxian Arsenal went to the freshly secured province of Hunan to aid in the creation of a new light arms plant at Zhuzhou, expanding on previous arsenals first established by Tan Yankai in 1912 and revitalised by Zhao Hengti in 1924. While their goals of creating a cutting edge facility fell short due to the instability Hunan faced following the collapse of the League, Hunan sits at the crossroads of the nation and in turn it is imperative that we invest in expanding existing facilities there accordingly.

Expand Lanzhou Arsenal
In the ailing days of the Qing, Governor Zuo Zongtang of Shaanxi and Gansu elected to establish the Lanzhou Machinery Bureau in 1871. Hiring local craftsmen and purchasing the necessary machinery from abroad, he laid the foundations for a reasonably successful regional ordinance production centre by coordinating facilities under his command in Lanzhou and Xi'an. The rifles, artillery guns, and their corresponding ammunition allowed Zuo to achieve successes fighting across China's western reaches, though the plant was unfortunately shut down in 1882.\n\nMore recent warlords have reopened it, albeit much of the infrastructure and personnel were since lost or grown outdated. Still, sitting deep in China's interior, it could not hurt to develop an alternative source of artillery and indeed it seems our predecessors had a similar idea though they failed to see it to full completion.
Expand Artillery Production at [FROM.GetName]
[FROM.GetName] has the potential to become quite a centre for light arms production, churning out a sizeable amount of heavy guns and artillery shells to support our growing forces. It is critical that we make the most out of the region and its expertise for the good of the nation.

Integrate Yingkou Naval Yard
The port of Yingkou sits in a strategic site in the Yellow Sea, positioned in a controlling position along the Daliao river and Liaodong Bay. With Dalian (Port Arthur) under Japanese control, Yingkou was effectively the sole port under the Fengtian clique's direct control and significant (albeit insufficient) funding was provided to expand it as the home of their fleet. It is clear they have learned plenty from their Japanese partners, a skill-set we must be able to utilise for developing a fleet of our own.
Integrate Jiangnan Shipyards
Jiangnan Shipyard was founded in 1865 to build, repair, and covert both military and civilian ships alike. Located near the Yangtze and situated largely within the mighty port of Shanghai, Jiangnan is one of the premier shipbuilding centres in all of China. Long strongly under the control of the Beiyang government, the creation of the Legation Cities altered the dynamic significantly. Still, they have benefited from outside investment and expertise, something we must bring under our control for a more equal future.
Integrate the Fuzhou Arsenal
Fuzhou Arsenal, also known as the Foochow Arsenal or Mawei Arsenal, was created under the orders of late Qing Dynasty leaders Li Hongzhang and Zuo Zongtang a few miles up the Min River. Taking advantage of Fujian's proud naval heritage and local naval academies, the Fuzhou arsenal complimented the region by providing a western designed naval dockyard, a metal-working forge, and additional schools for navigation and marine engineering. Damaged in ensuing wars, it was rebuilt and remained a prominent dockyard though under Legation control. Having secured its return, we must utilise it to its fullest capacity in helping us build a modern navy.
Integrate Whampoa Dock Company
The Hong Kong and Whampoa Dock Company was founded by Douglas Lapraik and Thomas Sutherland in 1866. Often abbreviated Whampoa Dock or Kowloon Docks, it effectively monopolised shipbuilding in the Pearl River Delta across the 1870s. The Canton region has traditionally been a centre for external trade, and its location made it both an asset and a target in the South China sea. We shall integrate these newly acquired facilities into our navy.
Integrate Dagu Shipyard
Established in 1880 by Li Hongzhang in Dagu, the Dagu Shipyard was initially tasked with only repairing ships for the Beiyang Fleet. Two years later, it began producing warships and in 1891 it even produced firearms like naval guns. Occupied in 1900 by the powers and returned to China two years later, the Dagu Shipyard remained prominent in the Warlord Era. It also was notable for producing European designed machine guns for the Ministry of Navy.

[FROM.GetName] has become quite a centre for naval construction, with a powerful collection of drydocks, shipyards, and training centres for naval personnel. Having become its newest masters, it is critical that we make the most out of their expertise for the good of the nation.

As one of China's most poverty stricken provinces, Guangxi is hardly an apt place for industrialisation. Refurbish the Shijing Arsenal
Established in the 11th year of the Guangxu Emperor's reign, the original Guangzhou Machinery Bureau was merged into the Zengbu Arms Bureau and became the Manufacturing East Bureau. A product of the Westernisation Movement, much of the factory's equipment and machinery has been managed by German engineers - a legacy that has yet to remain unchanged with Germany's influence in China prevalent following the German victory in the Weltkreig. Nonetheless, the arsenal is starting to show its age and we ought to provide our due diligence in maintaining and refurbishing the arsenal in order to accommodate more modern gun production - allowing us to produce more than just rifles but also light machine guns as well.

Evacuate Military Industry to the Hinterlands
As most of China's heavy industries are located around the coastal regions of the country, the threat of a war that will engulf our coastline puts our industries at risk. In this time of crisis, we will seek to move and relocate arms industries to the inner parts of the country which we control - in order to preserve their use for our war efforts.
Evacuate Civilian Industry to the Hinterlands
As most of China's heavy industries are located around the coastal regions of the country, the threat of a war that will engulf our coastline puts our industries at risk. In this time of crisis, we will seek to move and relocate civilian industries to the inner parts of the country which we control - in order to preserve their use for our war efforts.

Radicals
Independent Radicals and Revolutionaries\n§LThe revolutionary strain of radicalism within China has a fascinating history emanating from the timeless struggle of martyrs and heroes in the struggle against perceived injustices. Mobilising the fury of the masses, various revolutionaries have come and went, either peacefully or through fire and war. Largely coalescing into various parties such as the Kuomintang, or Young China Party, the revolutionary strain of thought is largely in contention with that of the reformists.\n\nThe allure of radicalism extends all across China however, from the steppe of the Northwest Frontier to the bustling streets of Shanghai, many cling to the ideals of a radically transformed China, either in the socialist egalitarian ideal, or in the strong nationalist flavour. Operating either under the radical elements of the Kuomintang, Young China Party, or Chinese Syndicalist party, the extremist groups refuse to partake in the Chinese Parliamentary system; seeing the newly proclaimed Central Government as merely a betrayal of the socialist revolution.§!\n
KMT
Chinese Kuomintang (Zhongguo Guomindang)\n§LThe Chinese Nationalist Party has taken on many forms in its marred history. Founded as the parliamentary organisation of the Tongmenghui in 1912 by Sun Yat-sen, Huang Xing, and Song Jiaoren, the Kuomintang would endeavour itself to faithfully serve as a parliamentary party within the early Beiyang Republic. After Song's assassination and Sun's subsequent multiple exiles however, the Party would be reorganised into the Chinese Gemingang, and later revived as the Guomindang on October 10th 1919. Dedicated to the Three Principles of the People as espoused by Dr. Sun Yat-sen, the Kuomintang aspires for the implementation of the Party-State, or Dangguo, and implement the Three-Stage National Revolution. Through the aspiration for the Three Principles of Minzu, Minquan, and Minsheng (Nationalism, Democracy, and Welfare respectively), they hope to see a new revolutionary republic.\n\nAttempting to unify the nation in 1927 with the Northern Expedition, the Kuomintang and its National Revolutionary Army would ultimately fail in its endeavour, and be forced to flee into the mountains of the MinGan insurgent zone, Guizhou Province, and into Paris alongside Moscow. Rife with infighting, the party has largely been split on the precipice of its Right Wing and Left Wings. Heralded by Hu Hanmin and the remaining army cliques in Guizhou, the \
CSP
Chinese Syndicalist Party (Zhongguo Gongtuandang)\n§LLargely confined to urban centres or port cities, the Chinese Syndicalist Party for years remained merely a fragmented junior party of the Chinese Kuomintang. Considered to be milquetoast radicals espousing a foreign ideology in a nation without the large worker support base akin to that in Europe or the Americas, the Chinese Syndicalist Party still maintained significant successes in its political history. With key action in historical events such as the Hong Kong-Canton strikes in 1925, which resulted in the founding of the League of Chinese Syndicalists, or the Shanghai Uprising in 1932 which resulted in broad national mass action.\n\nWith its inherently pluralistic nature, the Chinese Syndicalist Party has found itself fragmented with a series of disparate cliques such as the Orthodox faction of Chen Duxiu and Li Lisan, the Radical faction of Chen Shaoyu, and other various syndicalists such as the Northern cliques of Li Dazhao, the Southern cliques of Tan Pingshan and Deng Zhongxia, and the regional cliques such as the likes of Cai Hesen in Hunan or Wu Yuzhang in Sichuan. Despite all of their pomp and posturing however, the CSP is hardly a major political force in China - with its main strength and influence only among radical students and workers.§!\n
FCDP
Federation of Chinese Democratic Parties (Zhongguo Minzhu Zhengtuan Tongmeng)\n§LFormed during the brief Zhili liberalisation after 1928, the United National Construction League would ultimately be banned but set the groundwork for the formation of the Federation of Chinese Democratic Parties. The FCDP attempts to unite all parties across the nation seeking constitutional, civilian democracy under its banner - including the Chinese Constitutionalist Party, National Salvation Federation, Political Science Clique, China Vocational Education Society/China National Democratic Construction Association, Democratic Socialist Party, the Crescent Moon Society, and numerous other parties.\n\nWith the unification of the nation and the solidification of political blocs, the unwieldy FCDP has seen much of its support siphoned off to regional or otherwise far more prevalent fully fledge parties. However in that vacuum it has incorporated various other minor parties to strengthen its position. Largely led by the Northern democratic parties and cliques, it has set itself as a primarily as an alternative to the southern dominated Grand Republican Alliance, balancing a conservative, reformist leadership with its more dynamic, grass-roots activists.§!\n
GRA
Grand Republican Alliance (Gonghe Datongmeng)\n§LThe Grand Republican Alliance is a quasi-legal political coalition of largely Southern Chinese parties operating in opposition towards the established Beiyang political order. Formed in 1931 after the swelling of anti-concessionist energy and nationalist agitation following the failure of the Zhili-Fengtian negotiation discussions, the Grand Republican Alliance would briefly ally with the retired Duan Qirui, and be joined by other \
Republicans
Independent Republicans and Intellectuals\n§LThe intellectual tradition within the Chinese political sphere has been a long, proud, and most certainly vocal one. Many participating politicians utterly and sincerely refused to participate in any political parties, seeing them as corrupt and restricting. Upholding themselves to their own principles and pragmatism as opposed to ideology, they are largely the gentlemen behind the \

DCP
Democratic Constitutionalist Party (Minzhu Xianzhengdang)\n§LAnother iteration in the long line of parties heralding the late Qing reformers like Liang Qichao and Kang Youwei, the Democratic Constitutionalist Party can be considered the successor to the Chinese Constitutionalist Party, and through that same standard of the Progressive Party, Research Clique, and so on. United in their drive for Constitutionalism and a functioning democracy, the Democratic Constitutionalists have adopted the mantle as the \
Regionalists
Independent Regionalists and Strongmen\n§LDefeat on the battlefield has not eradicated all vestiges of warlordism in China. Across the nation, there remain plenty of hangovers from destroyed major warlord factions, whether it be petty warlord cliques who submitted to new masters at the right moment, lower ranking subordinates who were hired by the new administration, or sometimes new strongmen who arose from the ensuing power vacuum and took advantage of their affiliation with the winners. The fact that many are merely feigning loyalty to the national cause to bully and exploit the people does not mean others are not genuinely popular - even the most avaricious men of the high warlord era were careful to build strong guanxi networks spanning military, civilian, and social circles. Charismatic, connected, and often corrupt, those that survived the changing order are masters at tapping into regionalist sentiment as well as discontent with the status quo, determined to outlast efforts to purge them. Only time will tell how many generations it will take to truly eliminate the harrowing legacy of the warlord era.§!\n
